The species was hunted for its threat to grain crops and for propensity for nesting in belfries until the mid-20th century. Particularly large numbers were culled in the county of <a href="/wiki/Norfolk" title="Norfolk">Norfolk</a>. Western jackdaws were also culled on game estates as they raid nests of other birds for eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In a 2003 dissertation on public opinion of corvids, Antonia Hereth notes that the German naturalist <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Brehm" title="Alfred Brehm">Alfred Brehm</a> considered the western jackdaw to be a lovable bird, and did not describe any negative impacts of this species on agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-hereth03_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hereth03-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The western jackdaw is one of a very small number of birds that it is legal to use as a decoy or to trap in a cage in the United Kingdom. The other pest species that can be controlled by trapping are the <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">crow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_jay" title="Eurasian jay">jay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">magpie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rook_(bird)" title="Rook (bird)">rook</a>. An authorised person must comply with the requirements of the <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_and_Countryside_Act_1981" title="Wildlife and Countryside Act 1981">Wildlife and Countryside Act 1981</a> and does not need to show that the birds were a nuisance before trapping them.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> As of 2003 the western jackdaw was listed as a potential species for targeted hunting in the European Union <a href="/wiki/Birds_Directive" title="Birds Directive">Birds Directive</a>, and hunting has been encouraged by German hunting associations.<sup id="cite_ref-hereth03_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hereth03-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Permission to shoot western jackdaws in spring and summer exists in Cyprus as they are thought (incorrectly) to prey on gamebirds.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadjisterkotis03_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadjisterkotis03-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_depictions_and_folklore">Cultural depictions and folklore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cultural depictions and folklore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Borrowed_plumes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Borrowed_plumes.jpg/220px-Borrowed_plumes.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Borrowed_plumes.jpg/330px-Borrowed_plumes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Borrowed_plumes.jpg/440px-Borrowed_plumes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="374" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Borrowed_plumes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harrison_Weir" title="Harrison Weir">Harrison Weir</a>'s 1881 illustration of a vain jackdaw wearing peacock feathers for "<a href="/wiki/The_Bird_in_Borrowed_Feathers" title="The Bird in Borrowed Feathers">The Bird in Borrowed Feathers</a>" (also known as "The Vain Jackdaw") fable<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-fable_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-fable-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>An ancient Greek and Roman adage runs "The swans will sing when the jackdaws are silent", meaning that educated or wise people will speak only after the foolish have become quiet.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>, a jackdaw can be caught with a dish of oil. A <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(mythology)" title="Narcissus (mythology)">narcissistic</a> creature, it falls in while looking at its own reflection.<sup id="cite_ref-wentworth_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wentworth-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The mythical <a href="/wiki/Arne_Sithonis" title="Arne Sithonis">Princess Arne Sithonis</a> was bribed with gold by <a href="/wiki/Minos" title="Minos">King Minos</a> of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, and was punished by the gods for her greed by being transformed into an equally avaricious jackdaw, who still seeks shiny things.<sup id="cite_ref-Graves1589_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graves1589-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> described jackdaws as harbingers of rain in his poetic work <i><a href="/wiki/Amores_(Ovid)" title="Amores (Ovid)">Amores</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-deVries76_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deVries76-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> notes how the <a href="/wiki/Thessalian" class="mw-redirect" title="Thessalian">Thessalians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">Illyrians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lemnian" class="mw-redirect" title="Lemnian">Lemnians</a> cherished jackdaws for destroying grasshoppers' eggs. The <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Veneti" title="Adriatic Veneti">Veneti</a> are fabled to have bribed the jackdaws to spare their crops.<sup id="cite_ref-wentworth_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wentworth-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In some cultures, a jackdaw on the roof is said to predict a new arrival; alternatively, a jackdaw settling on the roof of a house or flying down a chimney is an omen of death, and coming across one is considered a bad omen.<sup id="cite_ref-deVries76_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deVries76-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> A jackdaw standing on the vanes of a cathedral tower is said to foretell rain. The 12th-century historian <a href="/wiki/William_of_Malmesbury" title="William of Malmesbury">William of Malmesbury</a> records the story of a woman who, upon hearing a jackdaw chattering "more loudly than usual," grew pale and became fearful of suffering a "dreadful calamity", and that "while yet speaking, the messenger of her misfortunes arrived".<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Czechs" title="Czechs">Czech</a> superstition formerly held that if jackdaws are seen quarreling, war will follow, and that jackdaws will not build nests at <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1zava_(Bene%C5%A1ov_District)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sázava (Benešov District)">Sázava</a> after being banished by <a href="/wiki/Procopius_of_S%C3%A1zava" title="Procopius of Sázava">Saint Procopius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Swainson,_Charles_1885_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swainson,_Charles_1885-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The jackdaw was considered sacred in <a href="/wiki/Welsh_folklore" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh folklore">Welsh folklore</a> as it nested in church steeples&#160;– it was shunned by the Devil because of its choice of residence.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Nineteenth century belief in <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">the Fens</a> held that seeing a jackdaw on the way to a wedding was a good omen for a bride.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The jackdaw is featured on the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukrainian</a> town of <a href="/wiki/Halych" title="Halych">Halych</a>'s ancient coat of arms, the town's name allegedly being derived from the <a href="/wiki/East_Slavic_languages" title="East Slavic languages">East Slavic</a> word for the bird.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Laughter_and_Forgetting" title="The Book of Laughter and Forgetting">The Book of Laughter and Forgetting</a></i> (1979), <a href="/wiki/Milan_Kundera" title="Milan Kundera">Milan Kundera</a> notes that <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">Franz Kafka</a>'s father Hermann had a sign in front of his shop with a jackdaw painted next to his name, since "kavka" means <i>jackdaw</i> in <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">First recorded in <a href="/wiki/John_Lydgate" title="John Lydgate">John Lydgate</a>'s <i>Debate between the Horse, Goose and Sheep</i> (c.1430) as "A clatering of chowhis", and then in <a href="/wiki/Juliana_Berners" title="Juliana Berners">Juliana Berners</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Saint_Albans" title="Book of Saint Albans">Book of Saint Albans</a></i> (c.1480), as "a Clateryng of choughes".</span>
